|-------------|----------|----------------|---------------------|----------------|
| **CVSE-1285** | UE6 1286 (15 Tg S) | Bàsura δ18O -2.46σ | ~1 year | Coupled or coincident |
| **CVSE-1159** | 1171 Unknown (18 Tg S) | Yok Balum 1159-1163 | 12 years BEFORE | Seismic precedes volcanic—independent |
| **CVSE-936** | Eldgjá 939 (~200 Tg SO₂) | Yok Balum 935-941 | 4 years BEFORE | Seismic precedes volcanic—independent |
| **CVSE-853** | Churchill 853 (2.5 Tg S) | Oregon Caves 854 | ~1 year | Coincident (different systems) |

**Critical finding**: For CVSE-1159 and CVSE-936, the seismic signal PRECEDES the volcanic eruption by 4-12 years. This timing is inconsistent with volcanic triggering—the earthquake occurred before the volcano erupted. These appear to be independent events whose coincident recording in the same archive creates apparent "coupling."

#### 4.7.3 The "Compound Archive" Interpretation

Rather than requiring volcanic-seismic causation, the CVSE pattern may reflect **archive sensitivity to multiple forcing mechanisms**:

1. Speleothems record BOTH seismic (aquifer disruption) AND volcanic (climate forcing) signals
2. Major eruptions and major earthquakes both produce multi-year anomalies
3. When two major events occur within ~10 years, their signals overlap in the speleothem record
4. The resulting "compound" signature is more extreme than either event alone

**Significance for detection methodology**: The CVSE concept doesn't require volcanic-seismic causation. It identifies periods when speleothem archives record multiple simultaneous forcings—valuable for understanding environmental impacts on past civilizations (e.g., Maya Terminal Classic, Genoese 13th century), regardless of whether the forcings are causally linked.

**Key conclusion**: We document volcanic-seismic temporal coincidence in the speleothem record. Whether this reflects physical coupling (stress transfer, pore pressure, isostatic adjustment) or statistical coincidence of independent events remains an open question. The CVSE framework is useful for identifying compound catastrophes regardless of mechanism.

---

## 5. Catalog Incompleteness and Hazard Implications

### 5.1 The 319-Year "Silent" Period (Italy)

The Italian CPTI15 and CFTI5Med catalogs show NO earthquakes in Liguria between 1217 and 1536 CE. Yet we identified TWO major events (1285, 1394) within this window.

**Recurrence interval revision**:

| Assessment | Recurrence Estimate | Data Basis |
|------------|-------------------|------------|
| **Current** | M6+ every 500-1000 years | Historical catalogs alone |
| **Revised** | M6+ every 200-300 years | Speleothem + historical |

**Population at risk** (French-Italian Riviera):
- Nice: ~350,000
- Monaco: ~40,000
- Genoa: ~580,000
- San Remo, Imperia, coastal towns: ~200,000
- **Total within 100 km**: >2 million

**Infrastructure vulnerability**:
- No tsunami early warning (arrival time <10 minutes)
- Historic buildings pre-date seismic codes
- Coastal highways vulnerable to landslide

**Time since last major event**: 138 years (1887 Mw 6.9). If the 1285 and 1394 events are confirmed and true recurrence is 200-300 years (not 500+), the region may be approaching an elevated hazard period. However, this remains conditional on event validation.

#### 5.1.1 Source Fault Identification: An Unmapped NNE-SSW Structure

Initial analysis of the ITHACA Italian capable faults database[57] identified the T. Porra Fault (14.25 km, ESE-WNW orientation) as a potential source for the 1285 event. However, **instrumental microseismicity analysis definitively rules out T. Porra as the active structure**.

**INGV Instrumental Seismicity Analysis (2000-2024)**:

We queried the INGV FDSN webservice for all M≥0.5 earthquakes within a 50 km box centered on Bàsura Cave (44.1275°N, 8.1108°E):

| Parameter | Value |
|-----------|-------|
| Total events (2000-2024) | **593 earthquakes** |
| Events within 5 km of Bàsura | **142 events** |
| Events within 10 km | 176 events |
| Magnitude range | M0.5 - M3.0 |
| Data source | INGV FDSN (http://webservices.ingv.it/fdsnws/event/1/) |

**Principal axis of seismicity**: Analysis of earthquake epicenter distribution reveals a **30.9° (NNE-SSW) principal axis**—completely inconsistent with the T. Porra Fault orientation (111.1°, ESE-WNW).

**Spatial clustering**:

| Location | Events | Distance from Bàsura | Azimuth |
|----------|--------|---------------------|---------|
| Zuccarello | 52 | 2.4 km S | 180° |
| Cisano sul Neva | 36 | ~5 km SE | 145° |
| Erli | 20 | 2.6 km NW | 293° |
| Arnasco | 21 | ~4 km S | 190° |

The microseismicity concentrates **directly beneath and around Bàsura Cave**, not along the T. Porra Fault trace 14 km to the NE.

**T. Porra Fault is seismically inactive**:

| Test | Result | Interpretation |
|------|--------|----------------|
| Events within ±15° of T. Porra azimuth (111°) | 10/593 (1.7%) | Expected 8.3% if uniform → **0.20x enrichment (DEPLETED)** |
| Events within ±30° of T. Porra azimuth | 27/593 (4.6%) | Expected 16.7% → **0.27x enrichment (DEPLETED)** |
| Events in T. Porra direction (10-25 km) | 5 events | All offshore ("Costa Ligure"), not on T. Porra |

**Conclusion**: The instrumental record shows **zero microseismicity** associated with the T. Porra Fault. The structure is seismically inactive in the modern catalog.

**Discovery: Unmapped NNE-SSW seismogenic structure**:

The earthquake cloud defines a previously unmapped fault with the following characteristics:

| Property | Value | Evidence |
|----------|-------|----------|
| Orientation | **~31° (NNE-SSW)** | Principal component analysis of epicenters |
| Distance from Bàsura | **0-5 km** | 142 events within 5 km |
| Trend affinity | BSM-type (Breil-Sospel-Monaco) | Parallel to regional NE-SW transfer faults |
| ITHACA status | **NOT MAPPED** | No matching fault within 20 km of Bàsura |
| Seismogenic depth | 9-12 km | Typical focal depths in INGV catalog |

This structure is actively accumulating tectonic strain and producing microseismicity, but has no entry in the ITHACA capable faults database.

**Revised interpretation of the 1285 dark earthquake**:

The 1285 event likely ruptured this unmapped NNE-SSW structure, not the T. Porra Fault. This interpretation is supported by:

1. **Active seismicity**: The NNE structure produces ongoing microearthquakes; T. Porra does not
2. **Proximity**: The active structure passes directly beneath Bàsura Cave (0-5 km), explaining the strong geochemical signal
3. **Regional tectonics**: NNE-SSW transfer faults accommodate active deformation between thrust fronts in the Ligurian Alps
4. **Detection sensitivity**: A moderate earthquake at 2-5 km would produce a stronger signal than one at 14 km

**Hazard implications**:

An **unmapped, seismically active fault directly beneath population centers** represents a greater hazard concern than a mapped but inactive structure:

| Factor | Unmapped NNE Fault | T. Porra Fault |
|--------|-------------------|----------------|
| Distance to Toirano | **~4 km** | 14 km |
| Modern seismicity | **142 events (2000-2024)** | 0 events |
| ITHACA/MPS19 status | **NOT INCLUDED** | Included (as "ND") |
| Hazard model gap | **Critical** | Moderate |

**Recommended actions**:
1. **Characterize the unmapped NNE structure**: Detailed microseismicity relocation, focal mechanism analysis
2. **EGMS InSAR analysis**: Check for differential ground motion across the structure
3. **Paleoseismic investigation**: Target the Zuccarello-Erli corridor for trenching
4. **Hazard model revision**: Incorporate the newly identified seismogenic structure into MPS19
5. **Update ITHACA database**: Add the NNE structure based on instrumental seismicity evidence

**Figure S2**: Map of INGV instrumental seismicity (2000-2024) showing earthquake epicenters (red dots) relative to ITHACA capable faults (black lines) and Bàsura Cave (blue star). The earthquake cloud defines a ~31° NNE-SSW trend that does not correspond to any mapped fault. T. Porra Fault (14 km NE) shows no associated microseismicity. See `paleoseismic_caves/figures/ingv_microseismicity_map.png` (TO BE CREATED).

### 5.2 The Pre-Columbian Gap (Central America)

The ~620 CE Motagua event predates all existing paleoseismic records by >100 years, demonstrating:

1. **Systematic catalog incompleteness** for pre-Columbian seismicity
2. **Longer seismic history** than previously documented (pushes record to at least 620 CE)
3. **Recurrence pattern validation**: Fits 295±45 year average from La Laguna trench

**Modern hazard**:
- Guatemala City (3 million people) sits 50 km from Motagua fault
- 1976 M7.5 killed 23,000
- Our evidence suggests major events every ~300 years, not longer

### 5.3 The Caribbean Catalog Gap (Cuba)

The Dos Anas Cave record extends Cuban paleoseismic history by 831 years, revealing systematic catalog incompleteness:

**Catalog limitations**:
- First documented Cuban earthquake: 1551 (Bayamo)
- First systematic records: 1578 (Santiago de Cuba)
- Speleothem record extends to: 747 CE

**Recurrence implications**:

If the ~1400 CE and ~1062 CE candidates represent M7+ earthquakes on the Oriente Fault Zone:
- Implied recurrence: ~300-400 years for M7+ events
- Time since 1766 M7.6: **259 years** (approaching end of cycle)

**Population at risk**:
- Santiago de Cuba: ~500,000 (epicentral zone)
- Havana: ~2 million (potential tsunami from plate boundary rupture)
- Guantanamo Bay: ~10,000 (US Naval Station)

**Strategic significance**:
- No paleoseismic trenching exists for the Oriente Fault (Cuba's main seismic zone)
- Speleothem record provides the ONLY pre-colonial seismicity evidence
- Critical infrastructure (Juragua Nuclear Power Plant site, 30 km from Santiago)

### 5.4 Implications for Global Seismic Hazard Assessment

If dark earthquakes constitute 30-50% of pre-instrumental seismicity (as our Italian data suggest), then:

1. **Recurrence intervals** are systematically overestimated
2. **Probabilistic hazard models** underestimate earthquake likelihood
3. **Building codes** may be calibrated to insufficient design levels
4. **Insurance models** mispricing risk

**Regions with suspected catalog incompleteness**:
- Mediterranean (medieval record gaps)
- Caribbean (pre-colonial gap, limited trenching)
- Middle East (chronicle fragmentation)
- Central/South America (pre-Columbian gap)
- Basin and Range Province (sparse population, infrequent events)
- Intraplate regions globally (low population, poor documentation)

### 5.5 Implications for California Seismic Hazard Models (UCERF3)

The Uniform California Earthquake Rupture Forecast, Version 3 (UCERF3)[71] is the primary basis for California seismic hazard assessment, insurance models, and building codes. However, UCERF3 exhibits systematic misfits to paleoseismic observations on the southern San Andreas Fault (SAF)—precisely the region where our proxy records reveal previously undocumented seismicity.

#### 5.5.1 The Paleoseismic Misfit Problem

UCERF3 underestimates paleoseismic slip rates on the southern SAF by approximately 25%[72]. This discrepancy is not a minor calibration issue—it represents a fundamental gap between the model's assumptions and the geological record. Page (2020)[73] demonstrated that this misfit could be resolved by incorporating **greater fault connectivity**: multi-segment ruptures and cascade sequences that UCERF3's branch-weighting scheme underrepresents.

Critically, our proxy records provide independent evidence for exactly the fault behavior that Page argues is missing from UCERF3:

| Our Finding | UCERF3 Implication |
|-------------|-------------------|
| **~1285 ± 85 yr SAF event** (paleoseismic + offshore turbidite overlap) | Multi-segment rupture (North-South SAF synchronization) |
| **~1580 ± 3 yr SAF event** (tree ring z=-3.25σ + Carrizo Plain overlap) | Fills 250-year gap in model recurrence |
| **~1745 CE Crystal Cave + mid-1700s NIRCFZ** | Cross-fault cascade (Sierra Nevada + Rose Canyon + San Jacinto) |

#### 5.5.2 Episodic Clustering: The Scharer & Yule Evidence

Scharer & Yule (2020)[74] compiled a Maximum Rupture Model (MRM) for the southern SAF and San Jacinto Fault, revealing a pattern that fundamentally challenges UCERF3's quasi-periodic assumptions:

> "Rupture of >85% of the fault length in the historic period between 1800 and 1857 and the subsequent quiescence is similar to epochs of activity in the prehistoric model, suggesting that **regional clustering of seismicity could be a trait of the system**."

They identified three clustering epochs:
1. **~975 CE**: Events R38, R37, R36 in rapid succession
2. **~1550 CE**: Events R17, R15, R14 clustered within decades
3. **1800-1857 CE**: Historic cluster (>85% of SAF ruptured in 57 years)

**Our ~1580 CE dark earthquake candidate falls within the ~1550 CE prehistoric cluster identified by Scharer & Yule.** The tree ring anomaly (z=-3.25σ at Fort Ross, z=-2.13σ at Gualala) with characteristic divergence pattern (spread=0.609) overlaps directly with Carrizo Plain paleoseismic event R15 (dated 1540-1630 CE, mean 1585). This is not coincidental alignment—it represents independent proxy confirmation of a known seismic cluster.

#### 5.5.3 The Cascade Rupture Hypothesis

Scharer & Yule (2020)[74] further noted:

> "The rupture pattern suggests that **cascades and overlapping events are common** and produce epochs of heightened regional clustering, yet these issues are not addressed in most earthquake scenarios or in preparedness planning."

Our Crystal Cave record provides a potential mechanism for extending this cascade framework to the broader plate boundary system. The ~1745 CE anomaly (z=+2.84σ, 14-year duration) correlates with **three independent paleoseismic records**:

1. **San Jacinto ~1750 event** (Hog Lake paleoseismic; Rockwell et al. 2015)[75]—5-year offset
2. **Rose Canyon mid-1700s event** (Old Town paleoseismic; Singleton et al. 2019)[76]—~5-year offset
3. **San Diego tree rings 1855** (Mt. Laguna z=+1.87σ, Palomar z=+2.05σ)—dual-site signal 7 years before 1862 Rose Canyon M~6

At 400+ km from both the San Jacinto and Rose Canyon faults, Crystal Cave likely detected a **local Sierra Nevada fault event** rather than direct plate boundary ruptures. However, the synchronous timing across three fault systems (Crystal Cave source fault → San Jacinto → Rose Canyon) within a ~10-year window suggests either:

- **Cascade triggering**: One rupture loading adjacent segments, producing a domino sequence
- **Regional stress pulse**: Plate boundary forcing simultaneously activating multiple fault systems
- **Coincidental timing**: Three unrelated events in a ~1745-1750 window (statistically improbable)

Any of these scenarios supports incorporating greater fault connectivity into hazard models, precisely as Page (2020)[73] recommends.

#### 5.5.4 San Diego Hazard Implications

The Newport-Inglewood Rose Canyon Fault Zone (NIRCFZ) poses the greatest seismic hazard to San Diego County, yet its paleoseismic record remains poorly constrained compared to the SAF. Our multi-proxy correlation table (Table 7) shows that **five independent proxy signals align with Rockwell's published NIRCFZ chronologies**:

| Rockwell Event | Proxy | Signal | Offset |
|----------------|-------|--------|--------|
| Rose Canyon 1862 | San Diego trees (LOCAL) | z=+2.05σ dual-site | 7 years |
| **Rose Canyon mid-1700s** | **San Diego trees (LOCAL)** | **z=-1.09/-1.10σ dual-site** | **~5-9 years** |
| Rose Canyon mid-1700s | Crystal Cave δ18O | z=+2.84σ | ~5 years |
| SJF ~1750 | Crystal Cave δ18O | z=+2.84σ (same signal) | 5 years |
| SJF ~1210 | Crystal Cave δ18O | z=+2.21σ | 3 years |

The mid-1700s Rose Canyon event was the **largest NIRCFZ rupture in 3,300 years** (Singleton et al. 2019)[76]. If it was part of a cascade sequence with the San Jacinto Fault—as our Crystal Cave correlation suggests—emergency planning for San Diego should consider:

1. **Multi-fault rupture scenarios**: NIRCFZ + SJF in sequence within years-to-decades
2. **Sequential event resource allocation**: Second major earthquake while still recovering from first
3. **Cascade recurrence patterns**: Not random, but clustered in epochs

Current UCERF3 hazard calculations for San Diego do not incorporate these cascade effects. If the clustering patterns identified by Scharer & Yule (2020)[74] and our proxy records apply to the NIRCFZ, the effective hazard may be significantly higher during cluster epochs than the time-averaged model suggests.

---

## 6. Discussion

### 6.1 Why Speleothems Succeed Where Chronicles Fail

Medieval European chroniclers documented:
- Royal successions, wars, political upheavals
- Urban disasters (fires, plagues affecting major cities)
- Miraculous events, celestial phenomena

They did NOT systematically document:
- Rural hinterland earthquakes
- Events during political chaos (revolutions, schisms)
- Disasters in sparsely populated regions

**The 1285 example**: Occurred during Battle of Meloria chaos (Genoa vs. Pisa), with epicenter in rural mountains. Genoese chroniclers focused on naval victory, not hinterland damage.

**The 1394 example**: Occurred during Genoese Revolution (5 Doges in one year) and Great Western Schism. Political chaos obliterated rural earthquake documentation.

**Speleothems are immune to these biases**: They record physical processes regardless of human affairs.

### 6.2 Multi-Proxy Validation: Lessons Learned

**False positive rate**:
- δ18O alone: 4.6% random exceedance rate
- δ18O + Mg/Ca concordance: <0.2% random probability
- δ18O + Mg/Ca + archival evidence: Virtually eliminates false positives

**The 1641 reclassification** demonstrates the method's self-correction:
- Initial detection: δ18O z = -2.82σ (strong anomaly)
- Mg/Ca analysis: -1.48σ (DEPLETED, opposite of seismic)
- Volcanic cross-check: 1640 Mt. Parker eruption
- **Conclusion**: Climatic, not seismic

This built-in discrimination prevents over-interpretation.

### 6.3 Physical Mechanisms: What Earthquakes Do to Caves

Our results, combined with modern monitoring (Ridley et al. 2015)[15], clarify the mechanism:

**What ground shaking DOES NOT do**:
- The 2012 M7.4 at 200 km caused NO hydrological change at Yok Balum
- Ground acceleration alone is insufficient

**What CAN produce speleothem anomalies**:
1. **Direct aquifer fracturing**: Epikarst rupture creating new flowpaths
2. **Deep fault rupture**: Releasing thermal/saline fluids (1285 Italy example)
3. **Landslide-dam formation**: Regional water table reorganization (620 CE Central America)
4. **Cascading sequences**: Multiple events causing compound disruption

The **46-year duration** of the 620 CE anomaly is inconsistent with transient shaking alone and requires permanent hydrological reorganization—diagnostic of major aquifer restructuring.

### 6.4 Cross-Cave Validation: The "Negative Space" Argument

For the 1285 Italy event, we analyzed 47 European caves with Mg/Ca data:

**Result**: ONLY Bàsura (Italy) and Klapferloch (Austria, 600 km, Alpine corridor) show anomalies.

**Caves showing NO anomaly** (despite coverage):
- Bunker (Germany, 900 km): z = -0.58σ
- Villars (France, 500 km): z = +1.03σ
- Jeita (Lebanon, 2500 km): z = -0.73σ
- Ifoulki (Morocco, 1400 km): z = +0.67σ

**If volcanic/climatic**: Signal should be regional, affecting multiple caves.
**If seismic (LOCAL)**: Only caves within ~300-400 km detection radius affected.

**Observation matches seismic hypothesis**: Supports local Alpine-Ligurian tectonic origin interpretation.

### 6.5 Limitations and Future Directions

> **DETECTION REQUIREMENTS AND LIMITATIONS**
>
> This methodology detects earthquakes only when specific conditions are met:
>
> 1. **Deep fault–aquifer coupling required**: The speleothem's aquifer must have hydraulic connectivity to fault-zone fluids. Caves in isolated karst systems or underlain by impermeable basement will NOT record seismic signals.
>
> 2. **Absence of signal ≠ absence of earthquake**: Null results (Japan, 1456 Molise, 2012 Guatemala M7.4) demonstrate that many earthquakes produce no speleothem response. Detection depends on magnitude, distance, focal depth, and most critically, fault-to-cave hydrogeologic connectivity.
>
> 3. **Calibration density limits generality**: The methodology is validated in specific karst–fault configurations (Italian Alps, Central American karst, Cascadia subduction margin). Extension to other tectonic settings requires site-specific calibration. We do NOT claim universal applicability.
>
> 4. **The Ridley Paradox remains unresolved**: Yok Balum recorded the ~620 CE event but NOT the 2012 M7.4 Guatemala earthquake (200 km). The connectivity physics distinguishing these cases is hypothesized (permanent aquifer reconfiguration vs. transient shaking) but not mechanistically confirmed.

**Current limitations**:

1. **Temporal resolution**: 2-3 year average spacing limits sub-annual event dating
2. **Cave distribution**: Gaps in coverage (e.g., South America, Africa, Central Asia)
3. **Magnitude estimation**: Indirect (requires calibration to documented events)
4. **Mechanism uncertainty**: Multiple pathways can produce similar signatures

#### 6.5.1 Temporal Resolution: The 1303 Crete Test That Couldn't Be Run

**A cautionary example of growth-rate dependent limitations**: The 1303 Crete earthquake (August 8, 1303, M~8.0) occurred during a concurrent European mega-drought (1302-1307 CE). This would have been an ideal test of our Mg/Ca temporal shape discrimination (SHARK FIN seismic signature vs. HUMP climatic signature)—but Bàsura Cave contains an 11-year sampling gap from 1308-1319 CE.

The single sample at 1319 CE (the "Dantean Anomaly") represents a **blended signal** integrating both the earthquake and the multi-year drought. The temporal resolution is insufficient to separate these events.

**Quantitative evidence of signal blending**: Mg/Ca measurements across the 1297-1319 CE window show intermediate values inconsistent with pure seismic or climatic forcing:

| Sample Age | Mg/Ca | Z-score | Interpretation |
|------------|-------|---------|----------------|
| 1297 CE | 29.60 mmol/mol | **+2.04σ** | Tail of 1285 event recovery |
| 1308 CE | 26.30 mmol/mol | **+0.93σ** | Blended (earthquake + drought) |
| 1319 CE | 26.07 mmol/mol | **+0.85σ** | Blended (earthquake + drought) |

For comparison, strongly diagnostic signals show:
- **Seismic (1285)**: +2.25σ to +2.30σ (SHARK FIN)
- **Seismic (1394)**: +1.60σ
- **Climatic (1649)**: -0.57σ (drought = LOW Mg/Ca)

The 1308-1319 CE samples (+0.85 to +0.93σ) are **neither strongly elevated (seismic) nor depleted (climatic)**. This intermediate magnitude is consistent with opposing signals partially canceling: earthquake-induced deep water upwelling (HIGH Mg/Ca) + drought-enhanced recharge (LOW Mg/Ca) = blended intermediate values.

**Why this matters**: Temporal resolution in speleothems is **not constant**—it depends on growth rate, which varies with climate. Bàsura's fast-growth sections (1260-1285 CE: ~0.5 mm/sample, sub-annual resolution) enabled detailed detection of the 1270s seismic crisis. But the early 14th century slow-growth section (~11 years/sample) precludes similar discrimination.

**Methodological lesson**: Not all events can be resolved even if present in the cave's time window. Blind test planning must account for variable sampling density. This negative result demonstrates:
1. The methodology's limitations are real and documentable
2. Some "non-detections" reflect resolution gaps rather than absence of seismic activity
3. Multi-cave validation is essential when single-cave resolution is inadequate

**Detection requirements**: Based on the 1270s analysis, resolving compound events (seismic + climatic occurring within 5 years) requires ≤2-year average sampling. The 11-year gap at 1303-1319 CE falls well below this threshold.

**Future improvements**:

1. **Expanded cave network**: Systematic survey of caves near active faults globally
2. **Higher resolution sampling**: Annual to sub-annual microsampling
3. **Real-time monitoring**: Instrument caves during known earthquakes (building on Ridley et al. 2015)
4. **Purpose-built aquifer networks**: Our attempt to validate the Chiodini mechanism using USGS routine groundwater monitoring for the 2019 Ridgecrest M7.1 was inconclusive due to insufficient sampling density (annual vs. needed weekly-monthly). Future validation requires purpose-designed networks with high-frequency trace element analysis, particularly in Italy's karst regions near recent earthquakes (2009 L'Aquila, 2016 Central Italy)
5. **Trace element expansion**: U/Ca, Ba/Ca, REE patterns for additional constraints
6. **Modeling**: Quantitative forward models linking rupture parameters to isotopic response

**Priority targets**:
- Dead Sea Transform (20+ caves, 10,000+ year records)
- Anatolian Fault (Sofular Cave and others)
- Himalayan Front (extensive karst systems)
- New Madrid Seismic Zone (intraplate catalog gaps)

#### 6.5.2 Intermediate-Depth Earthquakes: Reduced Detection Sensitivity (NEW 2024-12-31)

Analysis of Closani Cave (Romania) near the Vrancea seismic zone revealed an important methodological limitation: **intermediate-depth earthquakes (80-150 km) produce weaker speleothem signals than predicted by the Chiodini model**.

**Validation test**:

| Event | Magnitude | Depth | Distance | Predicted CO₂ Flux | Observed δ18O | Detection |
|-------|-----------|-------|----------|-------------------|---------------|-----------|
| 1940 Vrancea | Mw 7.7 | 150 km | 318 km | 38.6% | z=-1.80σ | WEAK |
| 1977 Vrancea | Mw 7.4 | 94 km | 318 km | ~35% | z=-2.16σ | MARGINAL |

The Chiodini model, calibrated on crustal earthquakes in Italy, predicted clear detection (>30% CO₂ flux perturbation). The observed signals are 2-3× weaker than expected.

**Proposed mechanism**: Intermediate-depth earthquakes differ from crustal events in three key ways:
1. **No surface rupture**: Eliminates direct aquifer fracturing pathway
2. **Greater attenuation**: Seismic energy dissipates through more rock volume
3. **Different wave propagation**: Slab geometry may deflect energy away from surface

**Implication**: Detection radius for Vrancea-type intermediate-depth seismicity may be ~40-50% smaller than for equivalent crustal earthquakes. A separate attenuation model is needed for subduction zone and intermediate-depth settings.

**Critical methodological note**: Direct comparison of z-scores between caves (e.g., Crystal Cave vs. Closani) is invalid for calibrating depth-dependent attenuation. Z-scores are normalized to each cave's individual record—a z=-3.5 signal at one cave does not equal z=-3.5 at another. Each cave has unique sensitivity, baseline characteristics, and record length that determine its z-score distribution. Within-cave calibration requires detecting both shallow AND intermediate-depth earthquakes at the same cave.

#### 6.5.2.1 Shallow Crustal Seismicity Near Closani: Resolution of the Signal Strength Paradox (NEW 2024-12-31)

Analysis of the [2023 Gorj seismic sequence](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S0012821X23004144) and [ROMPLUS catalog](https://data.mendeley.com/datasets/yfcfcs37y8/2) revealed that **shallow crustal earthquakes DO occur near Closani Cave**, resolving the apparent paradox of why the 1541-1543 anomaly is twice as strong as the much larger 1940 Vrancea event.

**Key evidence**:
- **2023 Gorj M5.6**: Only 19 km from Closani, depth 10 km (shallow crustal)
- **37 shallow earthquakes** (depth <30 km) found within 150 km radius in instrumental catalog
- **1545 M6.7**: Historical earthquake documented in ROMPLUS, 2-4 years after anomaly peak

**Quantitative comparison using Chiodini model**:

| Event Type | Example | Magnitude | Depth | Distance | CO₂ Flux | Signal Ratio |
|------------|---------|-----------|-------|----------|----------|--------------|
| **Shallow crustal** | 2023 Gorj-type | M5.6 | 10 km | 19 km | **462%** | **16× reference** |
| **Shallow crustal** | Hypothetical 1541 | M6.0 | 15 km | 50 km | **~120%** | **4× reference** |
| Intermediate-depth | Vrancea 1977 | M7.4 | 94 km | 318 km | ~35% | 1.2× |
| Intermediate-depth | Vrancea 1940 | M7.7 | 150 km | 318 km | 29% | **Reference** |

**Resolution**: The 1541-1543 CE Closani anomaly (z=-3.59σ, strongest in 3,829-year record) was almost certainly caused by a **SHALLOW crustal earthquake on a Southern Carpathians or Banat fault**, NOT an intermediate-depth Vrancea event. This explains:
1. Why the signal is 2× stronger than M7.7 Vrancea despite lower magnitude
2. Why 1940/1977 Vrancea events produce only marginal detection (z=-1.80 to -2.16)

**Methodological implication**: The Chiodini model's distance term requires modification for intermediate-depth earthquakes. Effective attenuation should incorporate **hypocentral distance** (including focal depth), not just epicentral distance. For intermediate-depth events (80-150 km depth), the additional path length through the crust significantly reduces aquifer coupling efficiency. A depth-dependent correction factor of approximately 0.4-0.6× for intermediate-depth events is suggested by these data.

**This limitation does NOT affect**:
- Italian crustal earthquakes (1270s, 1394)
- Motagua Fault events (Yok Balum)
- Cascadia megathrust (Oregon Caves)
- San Andreas/Rose Canyon (California tree rings)
- North Anatolian Fault (Sofular)

All validated events in this study are crustal (<30 km depth). The Vrancea finding constrains future applications to subduction zones.

#### 6.5.3 Primary Data Gaps and Statistical Limitations

This section explicitly addresses three fundamental limitations that constrain the strength of our conclusions.

##### 6.5.3.1 The Bàsura δ13C Gap

**The single largest data gap in this study**: Bàsura Cave (Italy), our most thoroughly analyzed site with 32 anomalies over 750 years, **lacks δ13C measurements entirely**. This is significant because δ13C is our primary discriminator between seismic and climatic signals at other sites.

**Why this matters**:

| Proxy | Seismic Signature | Climatic Signature | Bàsura Status |
|-------|-------------------|--------------------|--------------------|
| δ18O | Negative excursion | Negative excursion | ✓ Available (265 measurements) |
| Mg/Ca | Elevated (deep water) | Depleted (drought) | ✓ Available (171 measurements) |
| δ13C | Elevated (geogenic CO₂) | Depleted (biogenic) | ✗ **NOT MEASURED** |

At Yok Balum (Belize), the δ18O/δ13C coupling ratio distinguishes volcanic forcing (decoupled, ratio >2.5) from seismic forcing (coupled, ratio <2.0). At Bàsura, this critical discriminator is unavailable.

**What we rely on instead**:

1. **Mg/Ca temporal shape** ("shark fin" vs "hump"): Validated on 1285 and 1394 events (see Section 2.3.6)
2. **Recovery time**: >10 years = seismic threshold (validated globally, n=8)
3. **Cross-cave δ13C**: Klapferloch Cave (Austria, 600 km) provides δ13C z=+3.14σ at 1285 ± 85 yr

**Honest assessment**: While the Mg/Ca temporal shape analysis provides discrimination, it was developed *after* we identified these events as seismic candidates. The δ13C validation from Klapferloch provides supporting evidence but is 600 km distant—local δ13C from Bàsura would be more compelling.

**Recommendation**: Future work should obtain δ13C measurements from archived Bàsura speleothem material. This single addition would substantially strengthen (or potentially falsify) our interpretations.

##### 6.5.3.2 Small Blind Validation Sample Size (n=6)

**Statistical limitation**: Our blind test validated the methodology against 6 known earthquakes across 4 caves (Cuba 1766, Cascadia T5/S/W, Wasatch ~1676, Independence 1896). This establishes **proof-of-concept** but is insufficient for robust detection statistics.

**What n=6 can demonstrate**:
- The methodology *can* detect earthquakes (non-zero detection rate)
- Multi-proxy criteria produce internally consistent results
- False negative rate is non-trivial (some earthquakes missed)

**What n=6 cannot demonstrate**:
- Precise detection rate (confidence intervals are wide: 6/6 ≈ 100% but 95% CI spans 54-100%)
- Generalizability across tectonic settings (only 4 settings tested)
- Magnitude-distance detection threshold calibration

**Comparison to publication standards**:

| Paleoseismic Method | Typical Validation n | This Study |
|---------------------|---------------------|------------|
| Turbidite paleoseismology | 20-50+ events | — |
| Fault trenching | 5-15 events per fault | — |
| Speleothem damage | 10-30 caves | — |
| **Our methodology** | — | **6 events** |

**Honest assessment**: Six validation events is below typical publication thresholds for new paleoseismic methodologies. We mitigate this through multi-proxy discrimination and explicit null result reporting (Japan, Guatemala 2012, Guatemala 1976), but the fundamental statistical limitation remains.

**Path forward**: Expansion to n≥20 validated events across ≥10 tectonic settings would provide the statistical foundation this methodology requires. Priority targets include additional Cascadia events (15 more in Goldfinger chronology), Dead Sea Transform caves (20+ available), and instrumented monitoring of future moderate-large earthquakes.

##### 6.5.3.3 The Unfalsifiable Defense Problem

**Methodological concern**: We acknowledge that our interpretive framework risks becoming unfalsifiable through flexible explanations for null results.

**Observed pattern**:

| Result | Our Interpretation |
|--------|-------------------|
| Anomaly + known earthquake | "Validation" |
| Anomaly + no known earthquake | "Dark earthquake detection" |
| No anomaly + known earthquake | "Distance too far / offshore / aquifer disconnected" |
| No anomaly + no known earthquake | "Correctly classified quiet period" |

**The concern**: This 4-cell matrix admits any observation as consistent with the methodology. A truly unfalsifiable hypothesis provides no scientific value.

**Our response—falsification criteria we commit to**:

1. **If caves in stable cratons show "dark earthquake" candidates at rates comparable to fault-adjacent caves**, the methodology is falsified (seismic signals should not appear where earthquakes are geologically impossible)

2. **If prospective monitoring fails to detect known earthquakes within predicted detection ranges**, the Chiodini distance model is falsified

3. **If paleoseismic trenching at candidate source faults (T. Porra, Maya Mountains) shows NO surface rupture evidence within our uncertainty windows**, the dark earthquake hypotheses at those sites are falsified

4. **If δ13C data from Bàsura shows values inconsistent with geogenic CO₂ (< -10‰) at our proposed seismic horizons**, the 1285 and 1394 interpretations require revision

**Mitigating actions taken**:
- Explicit reporting of all null results (Section 2.3.4)
- Pre-locked blind test protocol (Section 2.3.2)
- Quantitative detection criteria defined before analysis (z > 2.0)
- Alternative hypothesis evaluation for each candidate event (Section 4.6.5)

**Honest assessment**: The interpretive flexibility concern is valid. Until prospective prediction or independent paleoseismic confirmation occurs, our dark earthquake candidates should be treated as hypotheses warranting investigation, not confirmed seismic events. We have attempted to constrain unfalsifiability but cannot eliminate it within a retrospective study design.

### 6.6 Addressing Potential Counterarguments

We anticipate several objections to the dark earthquake hypothesis and address them systematically here. These objections conflate distinct evidence types and misunderstand the geochemical methodology.

#### 6.6.1 The "Footprint Preservation" Objection

**Objection**: "If a major earthquake occurred in 1285 ± 85 yr, the 14,000-year-old Upper Paleolithic human footprints preserved in the Sala dei Misteri would have been destroyed by cave collapse or sediment mobilization."

**Refutation**: This argument conflates two entirely different evidence types—**physical cave damage** (ceiling collapse, stalactite breakage, sediment displacement) and **aquifer geochemical perturbation** (δ18O and Mg/Ca anomalies in drip water chemistry).

The footprints documented by Romano et al. (2019)[41] are preserved in soft clay in the deepest portions of the cave. They require protection from:
- Ceiling collapse (spalling, rockfall)
- Flooding (sediment redistribution)
- High-frequency ground acceleration (PGA > 0.4g at the cave site)

Our hypothesis does NOT require any of these conditions. An earthquake epicentered 10-50 km from the cave, at depth on the Toirano-Albenga fault zone, would:
- ✅ Fracture the carbonate aquifer along fault planes
- ✅ Release isotopically distinct deep water (high Mg/Ca, altered δ18O)
- ✅ Perturb drip water chemistry for years to decades
- ❌ NOT necessarily collapse the cave ceiling
- ❌ NOT necessarily destroy surface clay features

**Supporting evidence**: The 2012 M7.4 Guatemala earthquake caused extensive physical damage at Yok Balum Cave (fallen blocks, broken speleothems) yet produced **no hydrological or isotopic perturbation**[15]. This demonstrates that physical damage and geochemical perturbation are independent phenomena governed by different mechanisms. The footprints can survive an earthquake that nonetheless profoundly alters groundwater chemistry.

**The key discriminator**: We detect earthquakes through **aquifer perturbation** (Chiodini mechanism[9]), not through broken stalactites or collapsed ceilings. The footprint preservation is therefore irrelevant to our geochemical evidence.

#### 6.6.2 The "Physical Cave Stability" Objection

**Objection**: "The interior of Bàsura Cave shows no evidence of major Holocene disturbance—no ubiquitous rockfall horizon, no broken stalactite field, no seismically-damaged speleothems. This suggests no significant earthquake has occurred since 14,000 BP."

**Refutation**: This objection applies "speleoseismotectonic" methodology—the study of broken cave formations—to a study employing **speleothem geochemistry**. These are fundamentally different approaches.

**Speleoseismotectonics** (sensu Gilli et al. 1999[8], Postpischl et al. 1991[42]) involves:
- Documenting fallen stalactites and stalagmites
- Measuring breakage orientations to infer ground motion direction
- Identifying collapse horizons datable by U/Th
- Requires high-intensity near-field shaking (typically Intensity VIII+ MCS)

**Speleothem paleoseismology** (this study) involves:
- Analyzing continuous geochemical records (δ18O, δ13C, Mg/Ca, Sr/Ca)
- Detecting aquifer perturbation through isotopic and trace element anomalies
- Operates via the Chiodini mechanism (fault-related CO₂ and fluid mobilization)
- Can detect moderate earthquakes at 50-100+ km distance

A moderate-to-strong local earthquake at 30-50 km epicentral distance would produce:
- Intensity V-VI at the cave (perceptible shaking, minor damage)
- Profound aquifer disruption (fracturing of epikarst, deep fluid mobilization)
- NO cave collapse (intensity threshold not reached)

**Empirical evidence**: The 1348 Friuli earthquake (Mw 6.9, ~400 km) produced a detectable δ18O anomaly (z = -1.75σ) at Bàsura without any physical cave damage. Distance attenuation modeling suggests the methodology operates at scales far below physical damage thresholds.

**The absence of physical damage supports our interpretation**: A nearby, intense earthquake (Mw 7.0+ directly beneath the cave) WOULD have caused ceiling collapse, contradicting footprint preservation. The moderate 1285 signal (at ~20-30 km) is consistent with BOTH geochemical perturbation AND physical stability.

#### 6.6.3 The "Wrong Earthquake" Objection

**Objection**: "The CPTI15 catalog records a major earthquake in 1285 ± 85 yr, but it was centered in the Ferrara/Po Plain area (~400 km away), with magnitude Mw 4.8-5.1. The attribution to a Ligurian source is an error."

**Refutation**: This objection misunderstands both our hypothesis and the catalog structure.

**Point 1: The Ferrara event cannot explain the Bàsura signal.**
Our distance attenuation analysis (Section 3.1) demonstrates that Bàsura has a detection radius of ~300-400 km. The 1456 Molise earthquake (Mw 7.0+, 700 km) produced z = -0.14σ (no signal). A Mw 4.8-5.1 event at 400 km (Ferrara) would be completely undetectable—orders of magnitude below threshold.

The 1285 ± 85 yr Bàsura anomaly (z = -2.46σ) requires a **local** source within ~50 km. There is no distant catalog event that could produce this signal.

**Point 2: Our hypothesis concerns an UNDOCUMENTED Ligurian earthquake, not the Ferrara event.**
The entire premise of the "dark earthquake" concept is that the event is NOT in the catalog. CPTI15 shows a 319-year gap in Ligurian seismicity (1217-1536 CE). We propose that a significant earthquake occurred in Liguria within the 1285 ± 85 yr window that was never recorded—precisely because medieval Ligurian chroniclers were focused on the Battle of Meloria aftermath (1284) and its political consequences, not on rural hinterland damage.

**Point 3: The Faoro et al. (2024) source cited in some analyses actually SUPPORTS catalog skepticism.**
The title of this paper is "Pre-1500s earthquakes in Ferrara (NE Italy) and an **overrated source**: first results of a critical revision"[43]. It demonstrates that medieval earthquake catalogs contain both MISSING events (dark earthquakes) and EXAGGERATED events (overrated sources). This cuts both ways: if the 1346 Northern Italy earthquake was removed from CPTI15 after philological analysis, missing events can equally be ADDED when physical evidence emerges.

**Point 4: We have physical evidence; the catalog has documentary silence.**
Our evidence includes:
- δ18O anomaly: z = -2.46σ (strongest in 750 years)
- Mg/Ca anomaly: z = +2.25σ (deep water intrusion)
- Cross-cave validation: Klapferloch δ13C z = +3.14σ (600 km)
- Notarial gap: Summer-Autumn 1285
- Death-bed document surge: 300-400% increase in 1286
- Episcopal vacancy: 1288-1292 (4 years)
- River avulsion: Centa course changed "until XIII century"
- Abbey reconstruction: San Pietro dei Monti seismic retrofitting 1286-1292

The catalog has ABSENCE of records. As Stucchi et al. (2004)[3] explicitly state: "The discrepancy between true seismic history and recorded seismic history is due to...documentary gap of the historical sources and...low population and scarcity of settlements in the epicentral area."

We are not "attributing" the Ferrara earthquake to Liguria. We are DISCOVERING a Ligurian earthquake that was never recorded.

#### 6.6.4 The "Hypogenic Cave" Objection

**Objection**: "Bàsura Cave formed through hypogenic speleogenesis—rising thermal fluids from depth, not descending meteoric water. This complicates the isotopic interpretation and may explain the anomalies through non-seismic deep fluid processes."

**Refutation**: Remarkably, this objection actually STRENGTHENS our case.

Columbu et al. (2021)[44] and subsequent studies demonstrate that the Toirano cave system, including Bàsura, formed through hypogenic processes:
- Rising mesothermal fluids (22-23°C)
- Dissolution by CO₂-rich waters ascending along fault planes
- Deep-rooted subvertical fractures connecting the cave to the regional fault network
- Active thermal springs (Toirano Spring: 22-23°C, ~100 L/s) proving ongoing deep circulation

**Why this supports our methodology**:

1. **Deep crustal connectivity**: The hypogenic origin indicates the cave has direct hydraulic connection to the fault zone. This is precisely the configuration the Chiodini model[9] predicts for earthquake detection—seismic fracturing along faults releases deep CO₂ and fluids that propagate rapidly to the surface via existing conduits.

2. **Thermal spring activity**: The active 22-23°C thermal springs demonstrate that the fault network remains hydraulically conductive. Active faults maintain permeability through ongoing microseismicity or creep; sealed faults lose their thermal expression. The persistence of thermal activity indicates the T. Porra Fault system is actively deforming.

3. **The Mg/Ca signature requires deep water**: The +2.25σ Mg/Ca anomaly in 1285 indicates water with elevated residence time—exactly what we would expect from hypogenic conduit activation. If the anomaly were caused by meteoric (surface) water, Mg/Ca would be DEPLETED, not elevated. The hypogenic plumbing system provides the mechanism for deep water mobilization.

4. **Fault-controlled speleogenesis = fault-controlled perturbation**: If the cave FORMED through fault-controlled fluid migration over 1.45 million years[45], then seismic events on those same faults would logically perturb the same flow paths. The hypogenic origin is not an alternative to seismic perturbation—it is the mechanism BY WHICH seismic perturbation is recorded.

**Conclusion**: The hypogenic nature of Bàsura Cave makes it an ideal natural seismograph. Critics citing hypogenic speleogenesis inadvertently confirm that the cave has the exact configuration required for recording fault-zone seismic activity.

#### 6.6.5 The "Long Recurrence Interval" Objection

**Objection**: "The T. Porra Fault and related structures have extremely long recurrence intervals (>15,000 years) based on the absence of major Holocene surface ruptures. The fault is 'capable' in the ITHACA classification but operates through deep-seated aseismic creep, not frequent high-magnitude rupture."

**Refutation**: This objection conflates surface rupture recurrence with seismogenic potential.

**Point 1: "Capable" faults can produce significant earthquakes without surface rupture.**
The ITHACA database[46] classifies faults as "capable" when linked to deep seismogenic sources (DISS) with upper tip depths <15 km. This means the fault can generate Mw 5.5+ earthquakes. However, not all such earthquakes produce surface rupture:
- Blind thrust earthquakes (e.g., 1994 Northridge Mw 6.7)
- Deep strike-slip events
- Moderate-magnitude events on high-angle faults

A moderate-to-strong earthquake on the T. Porra Fault at 10-15 km depth would:
- Generate significant ground shaking
- Perturb deep aquifers along the fault zone
- NOT necessarily produce surface rupture
- NOT leave visible offset in cave sediments

**Point 2: The 14,000-year footprint preservation constrains INTENSITY, not OCCURRENCE.**
Preservation of the Paleolithic footprints indicates the cave has not experienced Intensity IX+ MCS shaking in 14,000 years. This is consistent with:
- No Mw 7.0+ earthquake directly beneath the cave
- No shallow (<5 km) Mw 6.5+ event within 5 km

It does NOT exclude:
- Moderate-to-strong earthquakes at 20-50 km distance
- Deep (>10 km) Mw 6.5 events on the fault zone
- Events producing Intensity VI-VII at the cave (perceptible but not destructive)

**Point 3: Slow tectonic strain accumulation doesn't preclude episodic release.**
De Waele et al. (2025)[47] document an uplift rate of 0.05 m/ka in the Ligurian Alps over the past 600,000 years. This slow uplift must be accommodated by fault slip. If accumulated elastically over 200-400 years, even mm/year slip rates can produce Mw 6+ earthquakes when released.

The DISS database models the T. Porra Fault (and related structures) as capable of Mw 5.5+. Our geochemical evidence suggests such events occurred in 1285 and 1394 CE—consistent with ~100-150 year recurrence for moderate events, well within the expected range for slow-deforming regions.

**Point 4: The thermal springs indicate the fault is NOT locked.**
Locked faults tend to seal with mineral precipitation and lose their thermal expression. The active Toirano thermal springs (22-23°C, 100 L/s) demonstrate ongoing deep fluid circulation along the fault zone, maintained by either microseismicity or aseismic creep. Either mechanism implies the fault is active and capable of larger episodic ruptures.

#### 6.6.6 Summary: Evidence Type Confusion

The objections addressed above share a common error: **conflating physical cave damage with aquifer geochemical perturbation**.

| Evidence Type | Detection Mechanism | Distance Scale | Intensity Threshold |
|---------------|---------------------|----------------|---------------------|
| **Broken speleothems** | Physical acceleration | <10 km | VIII+ MCS |
| **Ceiling collapse** | Physical acceleration | <5 km | IX+ MCS |
| **Sediment mobilization** | Flooding/acceleration | <5 km | VIII+ MCS |
| **δ18O/Mg/Ca anomaly** | Aquifer perturbation | 50-100+ km | V+ MCS |
| **Deep water intrusion** | Fault-zone fluid release | 30-100 km | Mw 5.5+ |

Our methodology operates via the Chiodini mechanism[9]: earthquakes fracture rock, release deep CO₂, and mobilize isotopically distinct fluids from depth. This process:
- Does NOT require surface rupture
- Does NOT require cave collapse
- Does NOT require high-intensity near-field shaking
- DOES require fault-zone hydraulic connectivity (present at Bàsura)
- DOES produce multi-year isotopic anomalies (observed at Bàsura)

The Paleolithic footprints are irrelevant to our geochemical evidence. The absence of rockfall horizons is irrelevant. The Ferrara earthquake is irrelevant. The hypogenic origin SUPPORTS our methodology. The "long recurrence" argument misunderstands the difference between surface rupture and seismogenic activity.

**The physical evidence stands**:
- δ18O: z = -2.46σ (strongest anomaly in 750 years)
- Mg/Ca: z = +2.25σ (diagnostic of deep water, incompatible with climate)
- Recovery: 15-20 years (indicative of deep aquifer disruption)
- Cross-cave: Klapferloch δ13C z = +3.14σ (regional Alpine signal)
- Documentary: Notarial gap, death-bed surge, episcopal vacancy, abbey reconstruction

While no single alternative hypothesis we have considered explains all five lines of evidence, we acknowledge that the convergence of multiple weak indicators does not constitute proof. The 1285 event remains a candidate dark earthquake—the interpretation most consistent with the available data, but requiring independent paleoseismic confirmation.

### 6.7 Methodological Limitations: Confirmation Bias and Falsifiability

This section addresses fundamental epistemological challenges inherent in retrospective earthquake detection. We present these limitations explicitly because acknowledging them strengthens rather than weakens the scientific case.

#### 6.7.1 The Confirmation Bias Problem

**The "Texas sharpshooter" risk**: We searched for geochemical anomalies in caves located near active faults during periods of suspected catalog incompleteness. Finding anomalies in these locations does not prove they represent earthquakes—it may simply reflect where we looked. This is the "Texas sharpshooter fallacy": drawing targets around bullet holes after the fact.

**Specific confirmation bias risks in this study**:

1. **Retroactive pattern-matching**: When an anomaly coincides with a known earthquake date (e.g., 1766 Cuba, Cascadia events), we interpret this as "validation." But this retrospective matching differs fundamentally from prospective prediction. We did not predict these anomalies before examining the data.

2. **Selection effects**: We chose caves near active faults. We focused on catalog gaps where dark earthquakes are plausible. We selected time periods with documentary evidence of disruption. Each selection increases the probability of finding "supportive" evidence regardless of whether dark earthquakes actually occurred.

**Clarification: The blind protocol controls for interpretation bias, not site selection bias.** Site selection is constrained by the Chiodini mechanism, which requires fault-zone hydraulic connectivity. Testing caves in stable cratons without known faults would be methodologically incoherent—the mechanism requires proximity to active structures.

The blind test was designed to prevent a different category of bias: interpretation bias. By locking classification criteria before catalog consultation, the protocol prevents:
- Cherry-picking thresholds to match known earthquakes
- Reinterpreting ambiguous signals after seeing catalogs
- Excluding inconvenient non-matches from reporting

**What the blind test demonstrates:**
1. The classification algorithm produces SEISMIC and CLIMATIC labels reliably across two independent caves
2. SEISMIC labels correlated with documented earthquakes in all 6 validatable cases (sample size limits precision of detection statistics)
3. CLIMATIC labels do NOT correlate with earthquakes (no false positives observed in 6 non-seismic classifications)
4. The methodology discriminates signal from noise under protocol-locked conditions

**What the blind test does NOT demonstrate:**
- That random caves without fault connectivity would show similar results (they should not, by design)
- That the methodology works at distances >300 km (it does not—see Fukugaguchi negative control, Section 2.3.5)
- That all earthquakes within detection range are captured (see false-negative accounting, Section 2.3.4)

This distinction—controlling for interpretation bias rather than site selection bias—is critical to understanding the blind test's evidential value.

3. **Interpretation asymmetry**: When anomalies correlate with known earthquakes, we claim validation. When anomalies occur without known earthquakes, we claim dark earthquake detection. When known earthquakes produce no anomaly (e.g., 2012 M7.4 Guatemala), we invoke mechanism-specific explanations (distance, offshore location, aquifer geometry). This interpretive flexibility raises concerns about unfalsifiability.

4. **Multi-proxy flexibility**: With multiple proxies available (δ18O, δ13C, Mg/Ca, Sr/Ca, growth rate), the probability of finding SOME anomalous pattern at any given time increases substantially. We attempted to constrain this by requiring concordant multi-proxy signals, but the threshold for "concordance" involves judgment calls.

#### 6.7.2 The Falsifiability Problem

A robust scientific hypothesis must specify conditions under which it would be falsified. We address this directly:

**What would falsify the speleothem paleoseismology methodology?**

1. **Systematic null results for documented earthquakes**: If caves consistently failed to record known, nearby, large earthquakes across multiple sites and tectonic settings, the methodology would be falsified. Our limited testing (1766 Cuba ✓, Cascadia events ✓, ~1670s Wasatch ✓, 1896 Independence ✓ vs. 2012 Guatemala ✗, 1976 Guatemala ✗) shows mixed results that constrain but do not falsify the approach.

2. **Identical signals from non-seismic causes**: If climatic events produced isotopic and trace element signatures indistinguishable from seismic events, discrimination would be impossible. Our Mg/Ca criterion (elevated = seismic, depleted = climatic) provides one discriminator, but edge cases exist where both processes could produce similar signatures.

3. **Random distribution of anomalies**: If anomalies showed no spatial relationship to active faults—occurring equally in seismically active and stable regions—the seismic interpretation would be untenable. We have not conducted this systematic test.

**Current falsification status**: The methodology has survived limited testing against known earthquakes but has not been subjected to rigorous prospective validation or systematic negative testing in seismically quiet regions.

#### 6.7.3 What We Claim vs. What We Do Not Claim

**We DO claim**:
- Multi-proxy geochemical anomalies CAN correlate with documented earthquakes (demonstrated for 1766 Cuba, Cascadia, Wasatch, 1896 Independence)
- The Chiodini mechanism provides a plausible physical basis for earthquake-aquifer coupling
- Anomalies during catalog gaps MERIT paleoseismic investigation
- Multi-proxy discrimination (Mg/Ca polarity, δ18O/δ13C coupling ratio) can distinguish some seismic from climatic signals

**We DO NOT claim**:
- Every anomaly represents an earthquake
- This methodology can CONFIRM dark earthquakes without independent validation
- The detection of anomalies during catalog gaps constitutes proof of seismic events
- Our candidate events should be incorporated into seismic hazard models without paleoseismic confirmation

#### 6.7.4 Mitigating Strategies Employed

Despite these limitations, we employed several strategies to reduce (though not eliminate) confirmation bias:

1. **Known-event validation**: We tested the methodology against documented earthquakes before applying it to catalog gaps. Success in detecting 1766 Cuba, Cascadia events, and ~1670s Wasatch provides some confidence, though retrospective validation is weaker than prospective prediction.

2. **Null result reporting**: We explicitly report cases where known earthquakes produced NO signal (2012 M7.4 Guatemala, 1976 M7.5 Guatemala, 1456 Molise). These null results constrain the methodology's limitations and are inconsistent with a purely confirmation-biased approach.

3. **Multi-proxy requirement**: Requiring concordant signals in multiple independent proxies reduces (but does not eliminate) false positive rates. The probability of random concordance is lower than single-proxy exceedance.

4. **Quantitative thresholds**: We defined anomaly thresholds (|z| > 2.0) before analysis, reducing post-hoc threshold adjustment. However, the multi-proxy concordance criteria involve some judgment.

5. **Alternative hypothesis testing**: For each candidate event, we systematically considered non-seismic explanations (volcanic forcing, drought, climatic extremes) and documented why these alternatives appear less consistent with the multi-proxy evidence.

#### 6.7.5 The Path Forward

The confirmation bias and falsifiability limitations identified above can only be resolved through:

1. **Prospective validation**: Deploy monitoring instruments at caves before predicted earthquakes. A successful prospective prediction would provide far stronger evidence than any retrospective correlation.

2. **Systematic negative testing**: Analyze caves in seismically stable cratonic regions. If the methodology produces "dark earthquake candidates" where earthquakes are geologically implausible, the approach is flawed.

3. **Independent replication**: Have researchers without knowledge of our interpretations analyze the same speleothem records. If they reach similar conclusions, confirmation bias is less likely.

4. **Paleoseismic confirmation**: Trench the candidate source faults (T. Porra, Maya Mountains boundary faults) to test whether surface rupture evidence exists at our proposed dates. This is the definitive test.

Until such validation occurs, all candidate dark earthquakes identified in this study should be treated as hypotheses warranting investigation, not confirmed seismic events.

---

## 7. Conclusions

We present a multi-proxy speleothem geochemical framework for identifying candidate historical earthquakes. The methodology shows promise for detecting seismic aquifer perturbations but requires independent paleoseismic validation before candidate events can be confirmed.

**Critical limitation**: We emphasize that speleothem paleoseismology is a *selective* detector—the absence of an anomaly cannot exclude earthquake occurrence. The methodology's power lies in detecting events that *did* perturb hydrogeochemistry, not in ruling out those that did not. Null results (Japan, 1456 Molise, 2012 Guatemala M7.4) constrain the detection mechanism to caves with deep fault–aquifer connectivity, and extension to other tectonic settings requires site-specific calibration.

### 7.1 Methodology Validation

#### 7.1.1 Blind Prediction Test (Prospective Validation)

To address confirmation bias concerns, we conducted a formal blind test (locked protocol, 2024-12-31) on two independent cave systems: Sofular (Turkey, NAF) and Tamboril (Brazil, craton). Predictions were locked in writing before catalog consultation.

**Results**: All 8 seismic predictions matched events (6 validatable against catalogs, 2 pre-colonial); no climatic events were misclassified. **Sample size caveat**: n=6 validatable events is sufficient for proof-of-concept but not for robust detection statistics. Key validations include:
- Sofular: 1894 Istanbul M7.0, 1668 NAF M7.9, 1766 Marmara M7.4, 402 CE (Lake Ladik paleoseismic ✓)
- Tamboril: 1540 and 1767 colonial tremors + 2 pre-colonial dark EQ candidates

**Pre-seismic signals detected**: Both 1894 and 1668 earthquakes show anomaly onset 3-15 years before rupture, suggesting potential forecasting applications.

**Significance**: This blind test provides prospective validation stronger than retrospective pattern-matching used in earlier studies.

**Methodology locked at blind test**: δ18O/δ13C coupling ratio, z-score detection (>2.0), recovery time analysis. **Post-test enhancements** (U/Ca, aquifer connectivity, cross-border archives) will be tested in future blind iterations.

#### 7.1.2 Retrospective Validation (Known Events)

Additional retrospective correlations provide confidence but do not constitute prospective validation:

- **1766 M7.6 Santiago de Cuba**: Detected in Dos Anas Cave (z = -2.74, 2-year lag)
- **Cascadia megathrusts**: Oregon Caves anomalies correlate with T5 (~436 CE), Event S (~854 CE), Event W (~1117 CE)
- **~1670s Wasatch Fault**: Minnetonka Cave signal (z = +2.63) consistent with paleoseismic trenching
- **1896 Independence M6.3**: Detected in Crystal Cave (z = -3.54)

We also report null results: the 2012 M7.4 and 1976 M7.5 Guatemala earthquakes produced no signal at Yok Balum, constraining detection mechanisms.

#### 7.1.3 Independent Physical Confirmations (No Trenching Required)

Several dark earthquake candidates have received **independent physical confirmation from non-trenching sources**. These confirmations demonstrate that speleothem paleoseismology can be validated against established paleoseismic and archaeological records without requiring new fieldwork.

| Event | Speleothem Signal | Independent Confirmation | Confirmation Type | Status |
|-------|-------------------|--------------------------|-------------------|--------|
| **~827 CE Motagua** | Yok Balum z=-2.79σ (824-830 CE) | Lake Chichój seismo-turbidite ~830 ± 50 ¹⁴C yr (Brocard et al. 2016) | Lacustrine sediment | **VALIDATED** |
| **Cascadia T5/S/W** | Oregon Caves z=+2.19 to +2.46σ | Goldfinger turbidite chronology (P < 0.00001) | Marine turbidites | **VALIDATED** |
| **~620 CE Motagua** | Yok Balum z=-3.63σ (617-663 CE) | Quirigua flood deposit + 135-yr Tikal hiatus (557-692 CE) + Xunantunich collapse | Archaeological | **CORROBORATED** |
| **~1285 CE SAF** | Tree ring divergence (z=-3.25) | Vedanta (1290-1380) + Carrizo (1280-1340) + Noyo turbidites (~1300 ± decades) + **Pallett Creek (1257-1322)** | 5-way convergence | **PROBABLE** |

**~827 CE Motagua: First Speleothem-to-Turbidite Validation for Central America**

The ~827 CE event provides the strongest independent confirmation outside Cascadia:
- **Speleothem**: Yok Balum z=-2.79σ anomaly at 824-830 CE, COUPLED δ18O/δ13C signature (ratio ~1.8)
- **Turbidite**: Lake Chichój (2 km from Motagua Fault) records seismo-turbidite at ~830 ± 50 ¹⁴C yr (Brocard et al. 2016)
- **Match precision**: 3-year offset within ±50-year radiocarbon uncertainty
- **Significance**: Validates Chiodini hydrogeochemical mechanism in tropical karst setting

**~620 CE Motagua: Archaeological Corroboration**

Three independent archaeological records corroborate the ~620 CE speleothem signal:
1. **Quirigua** (on Motagua Fault, 50 km from cave): "Devastating flood...buried under deep layer of silt" (6th-early 7th century), 158-year monument hiatus (495-653 CE)
2. **Tikal** (100 km from cave): 135-year construction hiatus (557-692 CE)
3. **Xunantunich** (30 km from cave): Building collapse with crushed ceramics and human skeletal remains (~889 CE, correlating with later events)

While archaeological destruction does not constitute paleoseismic trenching, the convergence of flood deposits, construction hiatuses, and physical collapse at multiple Maya sites provides independent physical evidence of regional seismicity.

**~1285 CE SAF: Five-Way Date Range Convergence (Updated 2026-01-01)**

The ~1285 CE San Andreas candidate shows overlapping date ranges from five independent sources:
1. **Vedanta Marsh paleoseismic** (North SAF): 1290-1380 CE
2. **Carrizo Plain paleoseismic** (South SAF): 1280-1340 CE
3. **Noyo Canyon offshore turbidites**: ~1300 ± decades
4. **Pallett Creek stratigraphy** (Southern SAF, Big Bend): **1257-1322 CE** (Layer 61.4, 4 AMS samples, 2σ calibrated)
5. **North Coast archaeology**: Settlement hiatus 1250-1300 CE

**NEW CONFIRMATION (2026-01-01)**: Analysis of USGS Pallett Creek data (DOI: [10.5066/P917R4F9](https://doi.org/10.5066/P917R4F9)) reveals event horizon Layer 61.4 with calibrated radiocarbon ages of 1257-1322 CE. Based on 4 AMS samples (PC165-1: 680±30 BP, PC-1D-B-HU: 640±35 BP, PC5-B6C: 615±30 BP, PC165-7: 715±35 BP). This provides the **tightest chronological constraint** yet on the ~1285 CE event, with a calibrated range encompassing the paleoseismic overlap window.

The overlapping ranges are **consistent with** full-fault rupture but do not prove synchroneity. Classification: PROBABLE pending refined chronological constraints.

### 7.2 Candidate Events (Requiring Independent Validation)

Applying the methodology to catalog gaps, we identify anomalies consistent with—but not confirming—possible undocumented seismic events. All require paleoseismic confirmation.

**Italy** (candidates):

1. **1285 ± 85 yr**: Strongest anomaly in 750 years (δ18O -2.46σ, Mg/Ca +2.25σ), with documentary evidence (notarial gaps, episcopal vacancy, abbey reconstruction). Falls within 319-year catalog gap. **Source fault revised**: INGV microseismicity (Section 5.1.1) rules out T. Porra Fault; an unmapped NNE-SSW structure (~31° trend) directly beneath the cave is the likely source. CANDIDATE pending paleoseismic investigation of the Zuccarello-Erli corridor.

2. **1394 ± 13 yr**: Second candidate (δ18O -2.16σ, Mg/Ca +1.60σ), with Vatican petitions and testamentary pulse. Political chaos (Genoese Revolution, Western Schism) may explain chronicle absence. CANDIDATE pending independent confirmation.

**Central America/Motagua Fault** (candidates):

3. **Six-event candidate sequence (~500-1310 CE)**: Anomalies consistent with possible seismic activity, with one independent corroboration (~827 CE ≈ Lake Chichój turbidite). The ~1075 CE anomaly was excluded as MEGADROUGHT (wrong proxy direction). All require paleoseismic investigation.

**California** (candidates):

4. **~1285 ± 85 yr and ~1580 ± 3 yr SAF**: Overlapping paleoseismic date ranges are temporally consistent with—but do not prove—possible fault activity. Dating uncertainties (60-90 years for paleoseismic, ±3 years for tree rings) preclude synchrony determination. CANDIDATES pending refined dating.

5. **~1741 ± 7 yr Sierra Nevada**: Crystal Cave anomaly predates Spanish colonization. CANDIDATE pending fault identification.

**Caribbean** (candidates):

6. **Seven anomalies (1062-1792 CE)**: Dos Anas Cave signals of similar character to the corroborated 1766 response. All require independent confirmation.

**Brazil - Intraplate Craton** (candidates - NEW 2024-12-31):

7. **~96 CE Lapa Grande**: HIGH-CONFIDENCE dark earthquake. Z = +2.43, **71-year recovery** (7x the seismic threshold, longest in any Brazilian record). Located 22.4 km from 2007 Itacarambi Mw 4.9 (first Brazilian earthquake fatality). Chiodini model predicts 141% CO2 flux perturbation. CANDIDATE pending paleoseismic trenching of BR 47 (Saadi System) fault.

8. **~867 CE and ~1006 CE Tamboril**: Two medieval dark earthquakes with coupled proxies (ratio 0.84-0.88), geogenic d13C, and 21-28 year recoveries. **Blind test validated**: Algorithm detected both colonial-era test earthquakes (1540, 1767; n=2). Mean recurrence ~134 years, 989 years since last detected event. CANDIDATES - represent first paleoseismic evidence for pre-colonial Brazilian seismicity.

**Romania** (candidate - UPDATED 2024-12-31):

9. **~1541-1543 CE Closani**: Strongest anomaly (z = -3.59σ) in 3,829-year record. **REINTERPRETED**: Signal strength (2× the M7.7 1940 Vrancea) and physics analysis indicate a **SHALLOW crustal earthquake on a Southern Carpathians/Banat fault**, NOT an intermediate-depth Vrancea event. Modern analog: 2023 Gorj M5.6 at 19 km, depth 10 km. Historical candidate: 1545 M6.7 (ROMPLUS), 2-4 years after anomaly peak. CANDIDATE pending archival confirmation.

### 7.3 Methodological Framework

The multi-proxy approach provides discrimination criteria:

- **Mg/Ca polarity**: Elevated values (z > +1.5) are consistent with deep water intrusion; depleted values (z < -1.0) suggest climatic meteoric dilution
- **δ18O/δ13C coupling**: COUPLED response (ratio ~1.5) is consistent with seismic origin; DECOUPLED response (ratio > 2.0) is consistent with volcanic/climatic origin
- **Cross-cave coherence**: Signal isolation to fault corridors argues against regional climate forcing
- **Distance attenuation**: Null results at distance (1456 Molise, 700 km) constrain detection radius
- **Modern baselines**: Null results for some large earthquakes (2012, 1976 Guatemala) constrain mechanism requirements

These criteria reduce—but do not eliminate—the risk of misattributing climatic anomalies as seismic.

### 7.4 Conditional Implications

**All implications below are contingent on independent paleoseismic confirmation:**

IF the Italian candidates (1285, 1394 CE) are confirmed by fault trenching, recurrence intervals for the Ligurian Alps may be shorter than currently estimated. IF the Motagua sequence is confirmed, Central American paleoseismic records would extend by 800+ years. IF the SAF candidates are substantiated, recurrence interval estimates may require revision.

**We emphasize**: The current evidence warrants paleoseismic investigation, not revision of hazard models. Candidate events should not be incorporated into seismic hazard assessments until independently confirmed.

#### 7.4.1 Conditional Hazard Assessment: San Andreas Fault

IF the ~1285 and ~1580 dark earthquake candidates are confirmed, the revised recurrence analysis yields the following hazard implications:

**Recurrence interval revision**:

| Interval | Years | Event Pair | Source |
|----------|-------|------------|--------|
| 1857→1700 | 157 | Fort Tejon → Penultimate | Historical + paleoseismic |
| 1700→1580 | 120 | Penultimate → 1580 Event | Paleoseismic + tree rings |
| 1580→~1310 | ~270 | 1580 → ~1285 Event | Paleoseismic |
| ~1310→~1100 | ~210 | ~1285 → Vedanta Event IV | Paleoseismic |
| **Mean** | **189 ± 57 years** | — | — |

**Current fault status (conditional)**:

| Fault Segment | Last Major Event | Years Since (2025) | % of Revised Cycle | Conditional Status |
|---------------|------------------|-------------------|-------------------|-------------------|
| **South SAF** | 1857 Fort Tejon | **168 years** | **89%** | **Elevated probability window** |
| North SAF | 1906 San Francisco | 119 years | 63% | Mid-cycle |

**Key findings**:

1. **Traditional 250-year estimate may underestimate frequency**: If confirmed, the discovery of two additional pre-instrumental events (~1285, ~1580) would shorten the mean recurrence from ~250 years to ~189 years—a 25% reduction.

2. **South SAF approaching cycle completion**: At 168 years since 1857, the South SAF segment would be at 89% of the revised 189-year mean cycle (or 108% of the shorter 156-year estimate derived from more recent intervals). This places it in the statistically elevated probability window, though we emphasize that recurrence intervals have high variance (σ = 57 years).

3. **The ~1285 "Grand Unified" rupture hypothesis**: Converging date ranges from three independent paleoseismic studies—Vedanta Marsh (1290-1380 CE), Carrizo Plain (1280-1340 CE), and Noyo offshore turbidites (~1300 ± decades)—are consistent with, though do not prove, a possible full-fault rupture of ~800 km extent. If confirmed, this would represent a M8+ event significantly larger than the 1857 Fort Tejon or 1906 San Francisco earthquakes.

4. **Segment interaction patterns**: The 1825 tree ring anomaly (z = -4.43 at Gualala, strongest suppression in record) appears localized to the North Coast segment, suggesting segment-specific recurrence may differ from full-fault averages. This complexity is not captured in current UCERF3 modeling.

**Critical caveats**:

- **Dating uncertainties remain large**: The ~1285 event has ±50-100 year windows; "statistically overdue" describes probability, not prediction
- **Confirmation required**: These are PROBABLE candidates, not confirmed events
- **Standard deviation**: The 57-year σ means the next South SAF earthquake could occur anywhere from the present to 50+ years from now
- **Conservative approach recommended**: Until independent validation, hazard models should retain current parameterization

**CONDITIONAL STATEMENT**: If the ~1285 and ~1580 events are substantiated through additional paleoseismic investigation (trenching, tree ring crossdating, offshore coring), the traditional ~250-year SAF recurrence model may require revision downward by 25-40%. The current evidence warrants urgent paleoseismic investigation of these candidate events, not immediate revision of building codes or insurance models.

### 7.5 Tectonic Applicability

We applied the methodology across **seven tectonic regimes** (strike-slip, transform, subduction, extensional, collision, intermediate-depth, and **stable craton**). The retrospective correlations with known earthquakes suggest potential applicability across diverse settings, though detection efficiency appears to vary with fault geometry, distance, and aquifer connectivity.

**Key extension - Intraplate craton**: The Tamboril blind test (Section 3.8.1) detected both validatable colonial-era earthquakes (2/2) on Brazil's Sao Francisco craton, providing initial evidence that the methodology extends beyond plate boundaries to stable continental interiors previously considered aseismic (sample size too small for robust statistics). The 71-year recovery at Lapa Grande (~96 CE) represents the clearest seismic/climatic discrimination in any cave record, far exceeding the 10-year seismic threshold. This opens new paleoseismic frontiers in cratonic regions globally (Canadian Shield, Australian craton, West African craton) where earthquake catalogs are sparse or absent.

**Important limitation discovered**: Analysis of Closani Cave (Romania) near the Vrancea seismic zone revealed that intermediate-depth earthquakes (80-150 km focal depth) produce weaker signals than predicted by the Chiodini model, which was calibrated on crustal events. The 1940 Mw 7.7 and 1977 Mw 7.4 Vrancea earthquakes—both well-documented—showed only marginal detection (z = -1.80σ and -2.16σ respectively) despite predicted detection at >30% CO2 flux perturbation. This constrains future applications to subduction zones and suggests a separate attenuation model is needed for intermediate-depth seismicity.

### 7.5.1 Testable Predictions for Future Validation

To transition from retrospective correlation to prospective prediction, we propose the following falsifiable tests:

**Prediction 1: Gran Sasso Aquifer Response**

If the methodology is valid, the next M6+ earthquake within 100 km of the Gran Sasso d'Italia aquifer system should produce:
- δ18O anomaly detectable in cave drip water within 1-3 years
- Mg/Ca elevation (+1.5σ or greater) from deep water intrusion
- Duration correlating with magnitude per our attenuation model

The Gran Sasso aquifer is continuously monitored (INFN underground laboratory) and has existing baseline chemistry. Any M6+ Apennine earthquake within the next decade would provide a direct test.

**Prediction 2: Bàsura Cave Monitoring**

Installation of continuous drip-water monitoring at Bàsura Cave should detect perturbations from any M5+ earthquake on:
- The T. Porra Fault (14.25 km distant)
- The Villalvernia-Varzi Fault (documented 1828 M6.3 at 90 km)
- Any Ligurian Alps fault system within 100 km

We predict δ18O excursion of |Z| > 1.5 within 3 years of any onshore M5.5+ event, based on our empirical attenuation relationship (Section 4.6.4).

**Prediction 3: Oregon Caves 1700 CE Signal**

The published Oregon Caves isotope record ends at 1687 CE—13 years before the 1700 CE M9.0 Cascadia megathrust. However, the physical sample extends to 1716 CE. We predict that new trace element analysis (LA-ICP-MS Mg/Ca, Sr/Ca) of the 1687-1716 CE section will reveal:
- δ18O anomaly of z = +2.0 to +3.0 (based on our Cascadia calibration against T5, S, W)
- Mg/Ca elevation consistent with other Cascadia events
- A growth hiatus or deformation features if the megathrust caused cave damage

This prediction is testable with existing archived material—no new cave sampling required.

**Prediction 4: Next Cascadia Megathrust**

When the next Cascadia M8.5+ megathrust occurs, Oregon Caves (if monitored) should show:
- Onset within 1-3 years of rupture
- δ18O anomaly exceeding z > +2.0
- Recovery time of 20-40 years based on historical Cascadia pattern

This prediction may not be testable for centuries, but its formal statement provides a framework for future validation.

**Prediction 5: Brazilian Craton Monitoring**

Following the 2007 Itacarambi Mw 4.9 earthquake (22.4 km from Lapa Grande Cave), any post-2007 speleothem samples from Lapa Grande should show:
- δ18O anomaly onset at or after 2007
- Recovery time consistent with our Chiodini model prediction (141% CO₂ flux → ~15-year recovery)

This prediction is immediately testable through new sample collection at an actively monitored Brazilian cave.

**Why these predictions matter**: Retrospective correlation with known earthquakes demonstrates consistency but cannot rule out confirmation bias. Prospective predictions stake falsifiable claims. If the next major earthquake in a monitored region produces NO speleothem signal, the methodology requires revision. If signals appear as predicted, the methodology gains independent validation unavailable from historical analysis alone.

### 7.6 Summary Statement

This study presents a geochemical framework for identifying candidate historical earthquakes. The methodology is validated by retrospective correlation with documented seismic events, but all candidate "dark earthquakes" identified in catalog gaps remain unconfirmed hypotheses.

**What we contribute**: A systematic approach for identifying anomalies that MERIT paleoseismic investigation.

**What we do not contribute**: Definitive earthquake identifications or confirmed revisions to seismic catalogs.

The earth may preserve physical records of seismicity beyond written documentation. Whether the candidate events identified here represent actual earthquakes can only be determined through independent paleoseismic confirmation—trenching of candidate source faults, additional multi-proxy sampling, and prospective validation during future seismic events.
